[←8.3. Arrays and Termination](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/Arrays-and-Termination/#array-termination "8.3. Arrays and Termination")[8.5. Bounded Numbers→](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/Bounded-Numbers/#Fin "8.5. Bounded Numbers")
#  8.4. More Inequalities[🔗](find/?domain=Verso.Genre.Manual.section&name=more-inequalities "Permalink")
Lean's built-in proof automation is sufficient to check that `arrayMapHelper` and `findHelper` terminate. All that was needed was to provide an expression whose value decreases with each recursive call. However, Lean's built-in automation is not magic, and it often needs some help.
##  8.4.1. Merge Sort[🔗](find/?domain=Verso.Genre.Manual.section&name=merge-sort "Permalink")
One example of a function whose termination proof is non-trivial is merge sort on `[List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference")`. Merge sort consists of two phases: first, a list is split in half. Each half is sorted using merge sort, and then the results are merged using a function that combines two sorted lists into a larger sorted list. The base cases are the empty list and the singleton list, both of which are already considered to be sorted.
To merge two sorted lists, there are two basic cases to consider:
  1. If one of the input lists is empty, then the result is the other list.
  2. If both lists are non-empty, then their heads should be compared. The result of the function is the smaller of the two heads, followed by the result of merging the remaining entries of both lists.


This is not structurally recursive on either list. The recursion terminates because an entry is removed from one of the two lists in each recursive call, but it could be either list. Behind the scenes, Lean uses this fact to prove that it terminates:
`def merge [[Ord](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Ord___mk "Documentation for Ord in Lean Language Reference") α] (xs : [List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α) (ys : [List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α) : [List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α :=   [match](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Pattern-Matching/#Lean___Parser___Term___match "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") xs, ys [with](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Pattern-Matching/#Lean___Parser___Term___match "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference")   | [], _ => ys   | _, [] => xs   | x'::xs', y'::ys' =>     [match](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Pattern-Matching/#Lean___Parser___Term___match "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") [Ord.compare](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Ord___mk "Documentation for Ord.compare in Lean Language Reference") x' y' [with](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Pattern-Matching/#Lean___Parser___Term___match "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference")     | [.lt](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Ordering___lt "Documentation for Ordering.lt in Lean Language Reference") | [.eq](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Ordering___lt "Documentation for Ordering.eq in Lean Language Reference") => x' :: [merge](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#merge "Definition of example") xs' (y' :: ys')     | [.gt](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Ordering___lt "Documentation for Ordering.gt in Lean Language Reference") => y' :: [merge](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#merge "Definition of example") (x'::xs') ys'`
A simple way to split a list is to add each entry in the input list to two alternating output lists:
`def splitList (lst : [List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α) : ([List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α × [List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α) :=   [match](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Pattern-Matching/#Lean___Parser___Term___match "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") lst [with](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Pattern-Matching/#Lean___Parser___Term___match "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference")   | [] => ([], [])   | x :: xs =>     let (a, b) := splitList xs     (x :: b, a)`
This splitting function is structurally recursive.
Merge sort checks whether a base case has been reached. If so, it returns the input list. If not, it splits the input, and merges the result of sorting each half:
`def `fail to show termination for   mergeSort with errors failed to infer structural recursion: Not considering parameter α of mergeSort:   it is unchanged in the recursive calls Not considering parameter #2 of mergeSort:   it is unchanged in the recursive calls Cannot use parameter xs:   failed to eliminate recursive application     mergeSort halves.[fst](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.fst in Lean Language Reference")   Could not find a decreasing measure. The basic measures relate at each recursive call as follows: (<, ≤, =: relation proved, ? all proofs failed, _: no proof attempted)             xs #1 1) 70:11-31  ?  ? 2) 70:34-54  _  _  #1: xs.length  Please use `termination_by` to specify a decreasing measure.`mergeSort [[Ord](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Ord___mk "Documentation for Ord in Lean Language Reference") α] (xs : [List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α) : [List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α := [if](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") `unused variable `h`  Note: This linter can be disabled with `set_option linter.unusedVariables false``h : xs.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") < 2 [then](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") [match](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Pattern-Matching/#Lean___Parser___Term___match "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") xs [with](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Pattern-Matching/#Lean___Parser___Term___match "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") | [] => [] | [x] => [x] [else](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") let halves := splitList xs [merge](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#merge "Definition of example") (mergeSort halves.[fst](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.fst in Lean Language Reference")) (mergeSort halves.[snd](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.snd in Lean Language Reference"))`
Lean's pattern match compiler is able to tell that the assumption `h` introduced by the `if` that tests whether `xs.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") < 2` rules out lists longer than one entry, so there is no “missing cases” error. However, even though this program always terminates, it is not structurally recursive, and Lean is unable to automatically discover a decreasing measure:

```
fail to show termination for
  mergeSort
with errors
failed to infer structural recursion:
Not considering parameter α of mergeSort:
  it is unchanged in the recursive calls
Not considering parameter #2 of mergeSort:
  it is unchanged in the recursive calls
Cannot use parameter xs:
  failed to eliminate recursive application
    mergeSort halves.[fst](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.fst in Lean Language Reference")


Could not find a decreasing measure.
The basic measures relate at each recursive call as follows:
(<, ≤, =: relation proved, ? all proofs failed, _: no proof attempted)
            xs #1
1) 70:11-31  ?  ?
2) 70:34-54  _  _

#1: xs.length

Please use `termination_by` to specify a decreasing measure.
```

The reason it terminates is that `splitList` always returns lists that are shorter than its input, at least when applied to lists that contain at least two elements. Thus, the length of `halves.[fst](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.fst in Lean Language Reference")` and `halves.[snd](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.snd in Lean Language Reference")` are less than the length of `xs`. This can be expressed using a `termination_by` clause:
`def mergeSort [[Ord](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Ord___mk "Documentation for Ord in Lean Language Reference") α] (xs : [List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α) : [List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α :=   [if](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") `unused variable `h`  Note: This linter can be disabled with `set_option linter.unusedVariables false``h : xs.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") < 2 [then](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") [match](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Pattern-Matching/#Lean___Parser___Term___match "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") xs [with](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Pattern-Matching/#Lean___Parser___Term___match "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") | [] => [] | [x] => [x] [else](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") let halves := splitList xs [merge](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#merge "Definition of example") (`failed to prove termination, possible solutions:   - Use `have`-expressions to prove the remaining Reference")xs.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") [List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α := splitList xs⊢ (splitList xs).[fst](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.fst in Lean Language Reference").[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") [<](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LT___mk "Documentation for LT.lt in Lean Language Reference") xs.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference")`mergeSort halves.[fst](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.fst in Lean Language Reference")) (mergeSort halves.[snd](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.snd in Lean Language Reference")) termination_by xs.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference")`
With this clause, the error message changes. Instead of complaining that the function isn't structurally recursive, Lean instead points out that it was unable to automatically prove that `(splitList xs).fst.length < xs.length`:

```
failed to prove termination, possible solutions:
  - Use `have`-expressions to prove the remaining goals
  - Use `termination_by` to specify a different well-founded relation
  - Use `decreasing_by` to specify your own tactic for discharging this kind of goal
α:Type u_1xs:[List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") αh:[¬](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Not "Documentation for Not in Lean Language Reference")xs.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") [<](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LT___mk "Documentation for LT.lt in Lean Language Reference") 2halves:[List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α [×](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod in Lean Language Reference") [List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α := splitList xs⊢ (splitList xs).[fst](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.fst in Lean Language Reference").[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") [<](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LT___mk "Documentation for LT.lt in Lean Language Reference") xs.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference")
```

##  8.4.2. Splitting a List Makes it Shorter[🔗](find/?domain=Verso.Genre.Manual.section&name=splitting-shortens "Permalink")
It will also be necessary to prove that `(splitList xs).snd.length < xs.length`. Because `splitList` alternates between adding entries to the two lists, it is easiest to prove both statements at once, so the structure of the proof can follow the algorithm used to implement `splitList`. In other words, it is easiest to prove that `∀(lst : [List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α), (splitList lst).[fst](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.fst in Lean Language Reference").[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") < lst.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") ∧ (splitList lst).[snd](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.snd in Lean Language Reference").[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") < lst.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference")`.
Unfortunately, the statement is false. In particular, `splitList []` is `([], [])`. Both output lists have length `0`, which is not less than `0`, the length of the input list. Similarly, `splitList ["basalt"]` evaluates to `(["basalt"], [])`, and `["basalt"]` is not shorter than `["basalt"]`. However, `splitList ["basalt", "granite"]` evaluates to `(["basalt"], ["granite"])`, and both of these output lists are shorter than the input list.
It turns out that the lengths of the output lists are always less than or equal to the length of the input list, but they are only strictly shorter when the input list contains at least two entries. It turns out to be easiest to prove the former statement, then extend it to the latter statement. Begin with a theorem statement:
`theorem [splitList_shorter_le](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#splitList_shorter_le "Definition of example") (lst : [List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α) :     (splitList lst).[fst](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.fst in Lean Language Reference").[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") ≤ lst.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") ∧       (splitList lst).[snd](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.snd in Lean Language Reference").[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") ≤ lst.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") := `unsolved goals α:Type u_1lst:[List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α⊢ (splitList
```
unsolved goals
α:Type u_1lst:[List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α⊢ (splitList lst).[fst](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.fst in Lean Language Reference").[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") [≤](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LE___mk "Documentation for LE.le in Lean Language Reference") lst.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") [∧](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#And___intro "Documentation for And in Lean Language Reference") (splitList lst).[snd](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.snd in Lean Language Reference").[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") [≤](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LE___mk "Documentation for LE.le in Lean Language Reference") lst.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference")
```

Because `splitList` is structurally recursive on the list, the proof should use induction. The structural recursion in `splitList` fits a proof by induction perfectly: the base case of the induction matches the base case of the recursion, and the inductive step matches the recursive call. The `induction` tactic gives two goals:
`theorem [splitList_shorter_le](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#splitList_shorter_le "Definition of example") (lst : xs[)](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List.cons in Lean Language Reference").[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference")

The goal for the `[nil](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List.nil in Lean Language Reference")` case can be proved by invoking the simplifier and instructing it to unfold the definition of `splitList`, because the length of the empty list is less than or equal to the length of the empty list. Similarly, simplifying with `splitList` in the `[cons](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List.cons in Lean Language Reference")` case places `[Nat.succ](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat.succ in Lean Language Reference")` around the lengths in the goal:

[+](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HAdd___mk "Documentation for HAdd.hAdd in Lean Language Reference") 1

This is because the call to `[List.length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference")` consumes the head of the list `x :: xs`, converting it to a `[Nat.succ](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat.succ in Lean Language Reference")`, in both the length of the input list and the length of the first output list.
Writing `A ∧ B` in Lean is short for `[And](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#And___intro "Documentation for And in Lean Language Reference") A B`. `And` is a structure type in the `Prop` universe:
`structure And (a b : Prop) : Prop where   intro ::   left : a   right : b`
In other words, a proof of `A ∧ B` consists of the `[And.intro](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#AndDef___And___intro "Definition of example")` constructor applied to a proof of `A` in the `left` field and a proof of `B` in the `right` field.
The `cases` tactic allows a proof to consider each constructor of a datatype or each potential proof of a proposition in turn. It corresponds to a `match` expression without recursion. Using `cases` on a structure results in the structure being broken apart, with an assumption added for each field of the structure, just as a pattern match expression extracts the field of a structure for use in a program. Because structures have only one constructor, using `cases` on a structure does not result in additional goals.
Because `ih` is a proof of `List.length (splitList xs).fst ≤ List.length xs ∧ List.length (splitList xs).snd ≤ List.length xs`, using `[cases](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#cases "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference") ih` results in an assumption that `List.length (splitList xs).fst ≤ List.length xs` and an assumption that `List.length (splitList xs).snd ≤ List.length xs`:
`theorem [splitList_shorter_le](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#splitList_shorter_le "Definition of example") (lst : [List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α) :     (splitList lst).[fst](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.fst in Lean Language Reference").[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") ≤ lst.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") ∧       (splitList lst).[snd](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.snd in Lean Language Reference").[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") ≤ lst.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") := byα:Type u_1lst:[List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α⊢ (splitList lst).[fst](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Tuples/#Prod___mk "Documentation for Prod.fst in Lean Language Because the goal of the proof is also an `And`, the `constructor` tactic can be used to apply `[And.intro](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#AndDef___And___intro "Definition of example")`, resulting in a goal for each argument:


The `left` goal is identical to the `left✝` assumption, so the `assumption` tactic dispatches it:

The `right` goal resembles the `right✝` assumption, except the goal adds a `+ 1` only to the length of the input list. It's time to prove that the inequality holds.
###  8.4.2.1. Adding One to the Greater Side[🔗](find/?domain=Verso.Genre.Manual.section&name=le-succ-of-le "Permalink")
The inequality needed to prove `[splitList_shorter_le](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#splitList_shorter_le "Definition of example")` is `∀(n m : [Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")), n ≤ m → n ≤ m + 1`. The incoming assumption that `n ≤ m` essentially tracks the difference between `n` and `m` in the number of `[Nat.le.step](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___le___refl "Documentation for Nat.le.step in Lean Language Reference")` constructors. Thus, the proof should add an extra `[Nat.le.step](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___le___refl "Documentation for Nat.le.step in Lean Language Reference")` in the base case.
Starting out, the statement reads:
`theorem [Nat.le_succ_of_le](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#Extras___Nat___le_succ_of_le "Definition of example") : n ≤ m → n ≤ m + 1 := `unsolved goals n m:[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")⊢ n [≤](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LE___mk "Documentation for LE.le in Lean Language Reference") m → n [≤](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LE___mk "Documentation for LE.le in Lean Language Reference") m [+](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HAdd___mk "Documentation for HAdd.hAdd in Lean Language Reference") 1`byn:[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language [≤](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LE___mk "Documentation for LE.le in Lean Language Reference") m [+](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HAdd___mk "Documentation for HAdd.hAdd in Lean Language Reference") 1 [≤](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LE___mk "Documentation for LE.le in Lean Language Reference") m → n [≤](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LE___mk "Documentation for LE.le in Lean Language Reference") m [+](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HAdd___mk "Documentation for HAdd.hAdd in Lean Language Reference") 1`
```
unsolved goals
n m:[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")⊢ n [≤](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LE___mk "Documentation for LE.le in Lean Language Reference") m → n [≤](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LE___mk "Documentation for LE.le in Lean Language Reference") m [+](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HAdd___mk "Documentation for HAdd.hAdd in Lean Language Reference") 1
```

The first step is to introduce a name for the assumption that `n ≤ m`:
`theorem [Nat.le_succ_of_le](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#Extras___Nat___le_succ_of_le "Definition of example") : n ≤ m → n ≤ m + 1 := `unsolved goals The proof is by induction on this assumption:

In the case for `[refl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___le___refl "Documentation for Nat.le.refl in Lean Language Reference")`, where `n = m`, the goal is to prove that `n ≤ n + 1`:

```
unsolved goals
refln m:[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")⊢ n [≤](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LE___mk "Documentation for LE.le in Lean Language Reference") n [+](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HAdd___mk "Documentation for HAdd.hAdd in Lean Language Reference") 1
```

In the case for `[step](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___le___refl "Documentation for Nat.le.step in Lean Language Reference")`, the goal is to prove that `n ≤ m + 1` under the assumption that `n ≤ m`:

```
unsolved goals
stepn m m✝:[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")a✝:n.[le](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___le___refl "Documentation for Nat.le in Lean Language Reference") m✝ih:n [≤](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LE___mk "Documentation for LE.le in Lean Language Reference") m✝ [+](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HAdd___mk "Documentation for HAdd.hAdd in Lean Language Reference") 1⊢ n [≤](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LE___mk "Documentation for LE.le in Lean Language Reference") m✝.[succ](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat.succ in Lean Language Reference") [+](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HAdd___mk "Documentation for HAdd.hAdd in Lean Language Reference") 1
```

For the `[refl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___le___refl "Documentation for Nat.le.refl in Lean Language Reference")` case, the `[step](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___le___refl "Documentation for Nat.le.step in Lean Language Reference")` constructor can be applied:
`theorem [Nat.le_succ_of_le](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#Extras___Nat___le_succ_of_le "Definition of example") : n ≤ m → n ≤ m + 1 := byn:[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language The final proof is as follows:
`theorem Nat.le_succ_of_le : n ≤ m → n ≤ m + 1 := byn:[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language To reveal what's going on behind the scenes, the `apply` and `exact` tactics can be used to indicate exactly which constructor is being applied. The `apply` tactic solves the current goal by applying a function or constructor whose return type matches, creating new goals for each argument that was not provided, while `exact` fails if any new goals would be needed:
`theorem Nat.le_succ_of_le : n ≤ m → n ≤ m + 1 := byn:[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language In this short tactic script, both goals introduced by `induction` are addressed using `[repeat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/The-Tactic-Language/#repeat "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference") ([first](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/The-Tactic-Language/#first "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference") | [constructor](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#constructor "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference") | [assumption](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#assumption "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference"))`. The tactic `first | T1 | T2 | ... | Tn` means to use try `T1` through `Tn` in order, using the first tactic that succeeds. In other words, `[repeat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/The-Tactic-Language/#repeat "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference") ([first](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/The-Tactic-Language/#first "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference") | [constructor](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#constructor "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference") | [assumption](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#assumption "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference"))` applies constructors as long as it can, and then attempts to solve the goal using an assumption.
The proof can be shortened even further by using `[grind](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#grind "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference")`, which includes a solver for linear arithmetic:
`theorem Nat.le_succ_of_le (h : n ≤ m) : n ≤ m + 1:= byn:[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")m:[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")h:n [≤](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LE___mk "Documentation for LE.le in Lean Language Reference") m⊢ n [≤](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LE___mk "Documentation for LE.le in Lean Language Reference") m [+](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HAdd___mk "Documentation for HAdd.hAdd in Lean Language Reference") 1   [grind](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#grind "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference")All goals completed! 🐙`
Finally, the proof can be written as a recursive function:
`theorem Nat.le_succ_of_le : n ≤ m → n ≤ m + 1   | [.refl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___le___refl "Documentation for Nat.le.refl in Lean Language Reference") => [.step](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___le___refl "Documentation for Nat.le.step in Lean Language Reference") [.refl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___le___refl "Documentation for Nat.le.refl in Lean Language Reference")   | [.step](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___le___refl "Documentation for Nat.le.step in Lean Language Reference") h => [.step](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___le___refl "Documentation for Nat.le.step in Lean Language Reference") ([Nat.le_succ_of_le](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#Extras___NoTac___Nat___le_succ_of_le "Definition of example") h)`
Each style of proof can be appropriate to different circumstances. The detailed proof script is useful in cases where beginners may be reading the code, or where the steps of the proof provide some kind of insight. The short, highly-automated proof script is typically easier to maintain, because automation is frequently both flexible and robust in the face of small changes to definitions and datatypes. The recursive function is typically both harder to understand from the perspective of mathematical proofs and harder to maintain, but it can be a useful bridge for programmers who are beginning to work with interactive theorem proving.
###  8.4.2.2. Finishing the Proof[🔗](find/?domain=Verso.Genre.Manual.section&name=finishing-splitList-shorter-proof "Permalink")

The next step is to return to the actual theorem that is needed to prove that merge sort terminates: that so long as a list has at least two entries, both results of splitting it are strictly shorter.
`theorem [splitList_shorter](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#splitList_shorter "Definition of example") (lst : Pattern matching works just as well in tactic scripts as it does in programs. Because `lst` has at least two entries, they can be exposed with `match`, which also refines the type through dependent pattern matching:
`theorem [splitList_shorter](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#splitList_shorter "Definition of example") (lst : Simplifying using `splitList` removes `x` and `y`, resulting in the computed lengths of lists each gaining a `+ 1`:

Replacing `[simp](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#simp "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference")` with `[simp](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#simp "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference") +[arith](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Simplifier/Configuring-Simplification/#Lean___Meta___Simp___Config___mk "Documentation for Lean.Meta.Simp.Config.arith in Lean Language Reference")` removes these `+ 1`s, because `[simp](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#simp "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference") +[arith](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Simplifier/Configuring-Simplification/#Lean___Meta___Simp___Config___mk "Documentation for Lean.Meta.Simp.Config.arith in Lean Language Reference")` makes use of the fact that `n + 1 < m + 1` implies `n < m`:

The facts needed to prove that `mergeSort` terminates can be pulled out of the resulting `And`:

###  8.4.2.3. A Simpler Proof[🔗](find/?domain=Verso.Genre.Manual.section&name=splitList-shorter-le-simpler-proof "Permalink")
Instead of using ordinary induction, `[splitList_shorter_le](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#splitList_shorter_le "Definition of example")` can be proved using functional induction, resulting in one case for each branch of `splitList`:
`theorem [splitList_shorter_le](Programming___-Proving___-and-Performance/More-Inequalities/#splitList_shorter_le "Definition of example") (lst : While the second case looks a bit complicated, everything needed to complete the proof is present. Indeed, `[grind](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#grind "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference")` can prove both goals immediately:
`theorem splitList_shorter_le (lst : [List](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___nil "Documentation for List in Lean Language Reference") α) :     (splitList ##  8.4.3. Merge Sort Terminates[🔗](find/?domain=Verso.Genre.Manual.section&name=merge-sort-terminates "Permalink")
Merge sort has two recursive calls, one for each sub-list returned by `splitList`. Each recursive call will require a proof that the length of the list being passed to it is shorter than the length of the input list. It's usually convenient to write a termination proof in two steps: first, write down the propositions that will allow Lean to verify termination, and then prove them. Otherwise, it's possible to put a lot of effort into proving the propositions, only to find out that they aren't quite what's needed to establish that the recursive calls are on smaller inputs.
The `sorry` tactic can prove any goal, even false ones. It isn't intended for use in production code or final proofs, but it is a convenient way to “sketch out” a proof or program ahead of time. Any definitions or theorems that use `sorry` are annotated with a warning.
The initial sketch of `mergeSort`'s termination argument that uses `sorry` can be written by copying the goals that Lean couldn't prove into `have`-expressions. In Lean, `have` is similar to `let`. When using `have`, the name is optional. Typically, `let` is used to define names that refer to interesting values, while `have` is used to locally prove propositions that can be found when Lean is searching for evidence that an array lookup is in-bounds or that a function terminates.
``declaration uses 'sorry'`def `declaration uses 'sorry'``declaration uses 'sorry'``declaration uses 'sorry'``declaration uses 'sorry'``declaration uses 'sorry'``declaration uses 'sorry'``declaration ```

Once again, there is only a warning.

```
declaration uses 'sorry'
```

There is one promising assumption available: `h : ¬List.length xs < 2`, which comes from the `if`. Clearly, if it is not the case that `xs.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") < 2`, then `xs.[length](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Linked-Lists/#List___length "Documentation for List.length in Lean Language Reference") ≥ 2`. The `[grind](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#grind "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference")` tactic solves this goal, and the program is now complete:

##  8.4.4. Division as Iterated Subtraction[🔗](find/?domain=Verso.Genre.Manual.section&name=division-as-iterated-subtraction "Permalink")
Just as multiplication is iterated addition and exponentiation is iterated multiplication, division can be understood as iterated subtraction. The [very first description of recursive functions in this book](Getting-to-Know-Lean/Datatypes-and-Patterns/#recursive-functions) presents a version of division that terminates when the divisor is not zero, but that Lean does not accept. Proving that division terminates requires the use of a fact about inequalities.
Lean cannot prove that this definition of division terminates:
`def `fail to show termination for   div with errors failed to infer structural recursion: Not considering parameter k of div:   it is unchanged in the recursive calls Cannot use parameter k:   failed to eliminate recursive application     div [(](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HSub___mk "Documentation for HSub.hSub in Lean Language Reference")n [-](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HSub___mk "Documentation for HSub.hSub in Lean Language Reference") k[)](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HSub___mk "Documentation for HSub.hSub in Lean Language Reference") k   failed to prove termination, possible solutions:   - Use `have`-expressions to prove the remaining goals   - Use `termination_by` to specify a different well-founded relation   - Use `decreasing_by` to specify your own tactic for discharging this kind of goal k n:[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")h✝:[¬](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Not "Documentation for Not in Lean Language Reference")n [<](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LT___mk "Documentation for LT.lt in Lean Language Reference") k⊢ n [-](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HSub___mk "Documentation for HSub.hSub in Lean Language Reference") k [<](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LT___mk "Documentation for LT.lt in Lean Language Reference") n`div (n k : [Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")) : [Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference") := [if](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") n < k [then](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") 0 [else](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") 1 + div (n - k) k`
```
fail to show termination for
  div
with errors
failed to infer structural recursion:
Not considering parameter k of div:
  it is unchanged in the recursive calls
Cannot use parameter k:
  failed to eliminate recursive application
    div [(](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HSub___mk "Documentation for HSub.hSub in Lean Language Reference")n [-](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HSub___mk "Documentation for HSub.hSub in Lean Language Reference") k[)](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HSub___mk "Documentation for HSub.hSub in Lean Language Reference") k


failed to prove termination, possible solutions:
  - Use `have`-expressions to prove the remaining goals
  - Use `termination_by` to specify a different well-founded relation
  - Use `decreasing_by` to specify your own tactic for discharging this kind of goal
k n:[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")h✝:[¬](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Not "Documentation for Not in Lean Language Reference")n [<](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LT___mk "Documentation for LT.lt in Lean Language Reference") k⊢ n [-](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#HSub___mk "Documentation for HSub.hSub in Lean Language Reference") k [<](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#LT___mk "Documentation for LT.lt in Lean Language Reference") n
```

That's a good thing, because it doesn't! When `k` is `0`, value of `n` does not decrease, so the program is an infinite loop.
Rewriting the function to take evidence that `k` is not `0` allows Lean to automatically prove termination:
`def div (n k : [Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")) (ok : k ≠ 0) : [Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference") :=   [if](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") h : n < k [then](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference")     0   [else](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference")     1 + div (n - k) k ok`
This definition of `div` terminates because the first argument `n` is smaller on each recursive call. This can be expressed using a `termination_by` clause:
`def div (n k : [Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")) (ok : k ≠ 0) : [Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference") :=   [if](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") h : n < k [then](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference")     0   [else](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference")     1 + div (n - k) k ok termination_by n`
##  8.4.5. Exercises[🔗](find/?domain=Verso.Genre.Manual.section&name=inequalities-exercises "Permalink")
Prove the following theorems without using `[grind](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#grind "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference")`:
  * For all natural numbers `nnn`, `0<n+10 < n + 10<n+1`.
  * For all natural numbers `nnn`, `0≤n0 \leq n0≤n`.
  * For all natural numbers `nnn` and `kkk`, `(n+1)−(k+1)=n−k(n + 1) - (k + 1) = n - k(n+1)−(k+1)=n−k`
  * For all natural numbers `nnn` and `kkk`, if `k<nk < nk<n` then `n≠0n \neq 0n=0`
  * For all natural numbers `nnn`, `n−n=0n - n = 0n−n=0`
  * For all natural numbers `nnn` and `kkk`, if `n+1<kn + 1 < kn+1<k` then `n<kn < kn<k`
